Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1120;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 25;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 830;
    client_max_body_size 1245184;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.17.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'https://*.google.com';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1568;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 84;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 2043;
    client_max_body_size 748544;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 804;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 91;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 731;
    client_max_body_size 1828864;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.6.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1459200;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1577;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 43;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1871;
    client_max_body_size 849920;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

    keepalive_timeout 76;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;

    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;

    client_max_body_size 1961984;

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 663;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 57;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1610;
    client_max_body_size 698368;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.17.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1875;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 120;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1308;
    client_max_body_size 857088;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.17.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'https://*.google.com';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1903;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 73;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 934;
    client_max_body_size 1837056;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1126;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 110;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 540;
    client_max_body_size 1881088;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'https://*.google.com';";
        }
    }
}

worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1903;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 73;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 934;
    client_max_body_size 1837056;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;

        server_name www.exampletfm.com;

            deny all;

            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;

	 The Anti-MIME-Sniffing header X-Content-Type-Options was not set to 'nosniff'. This allows older versions of Internet Explorer and Chrome to perform MIME-sniffing on the response body, potentially causing the response body to be interpreted and displayed as a content type other than the declared content type. Current (early 2014) and legacy versions of Firefox will use the declared content type (if one is set), rather than performing MIME-sniffing.

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

	 The Anti-MIME-Sniffing header X-Content-Type-Options was not set to 'nosniff'. This allows older versions of Internet Explorer and Chrome to perform MIME-sniffing on the response body, potentially causing the response body to be interpreted and displayed as a content type other than the declared content type. Current (early 2014) and legacy versions of Firefox will use the declared content type (if one is set), rather than performing MIME-sniffing.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1460224;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1300;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 43;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1115;
    client_max_body_size 1586176;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.6.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'https://*.google.com';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy.

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 663;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 56;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1610;
    client_max_body_size 698368;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/7.2.1;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low
Alert →

Current (early 2014) and legacy versions of Firefox will use the declared content type (if one is set), rather than performing MIME-sniffing.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1610;
    client_max_body_size 698368;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.17.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 804;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 91;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 731;
    client_max_body_size 1828864;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1749;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 60;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 837;
    client_max_body_size 1961984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Alert →
	 http://www.exampletfm.com/test/upload.html 
	 <form action="../form" method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data"> 
	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 804;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 91;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 731;
    client_max_body_size 1828864;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.6.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }

    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1460224;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 837;
    client_max_body_size 1961984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

	 <form action="../form" method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data"> 
	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;

        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;

            deny all;

            deny all;

        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;

        }
    }

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1749;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 60;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 837;
    client_max_body_size 1961984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

	 <form action="../form" method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data"> 
	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1460224;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1190912;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;

        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }

            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;

        location / {
            root /tester/site/;

            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'https://*.google.com';";
        }
    }

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1190912;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;

            root /tester/site/;

	 http://www.exampletfm.com/test/upload.html 
	 <form action="../form" method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data"> 
	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/7.2.1;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1461248;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1460224;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1190912;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

	 <form action="../form" method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data"> 
	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1459200;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/7.2.1;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1061;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 2043;
    client_max_body_size 748544;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

}

    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
