        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;

    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1749;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 60;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 837;
    client_max_body_size 1961984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

    sendfile on;

    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 837;
    client_max_body_size 1961984;

    gzip off;

            deny all;

            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;

	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 884;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;

        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;

        }

            deny all;

        }

            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/7.2.1;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;

    worker_connections 1749;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 60;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 836;
    client_max_body_size 1961984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1460224;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

}

    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 33;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low
Alert →

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1568;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 84;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 2044;
    client_max_body_size 748544;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

	 The Anti-MIME-Sniffing header X-Content-Type-Options was not set to 'nosniff'. This allows older versions of Internet Explorer and Chrome to perform MIME-sniffing on the response body, potentially causing the response body to be interpreted and displayed as a content type other than the declared content type. Current (early 2014) and legacy versions of Firefox will use the declared content type (if one is set), rather than performing MIME-sniffing.

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low
Alert →
	 http://www.exampletfm.com/test/upload.html 
	  
	 The Anti-MIME-Sniffing header X-Content-Type-Options was not set to 'nosniff'. This allows older versions of Internet Explorer and Chrome to perform MIME-sniffing on the response body, potentially causing the response body to be interpreted and displayed as a content type other than the declared content type. Current (early 2014) and legacy versions of Firefox will use the declared content type (if one is set), rather than performing MIME-sniffing.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1903;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 73;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1010;
    client_max_body_size 1459200;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1568;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 84;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1459200;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1459200;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.6.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 884;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;

            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;

    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 33;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1459200;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }

            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.6.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1011;
    client_max_body_size 1460224;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.17.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low
Alert →
	 http://www.exampletfm.com/test/upload.html 
	 X-Powered-By: PHP/5.3.3 
	 The web/application server is leaking information via one or more "X-Powered-By" HTTP response headers. Access to such information may facilitate attackers identifying other frameworks/components your web application is reliant upon and the vulnerabilities such components may be subject to. 
	 Low
Alert →
	 http://www.exampletfm.com/form 
	  
	 Web Browser XSS Protection is not enabled, or is disabled by the configuration of the 'X-XSS-Protection' HTTP response header on the web server 
	 Low
Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/7.2.1;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1062;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 41;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.6.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

Alert →
	 http://www.exampletfm.com/test/upload.html 
	 <form action="../form" method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data"> 
	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1162;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 32;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 885;
    client_max_body_size 1189888;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}
